<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Chinese characters</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Logographic writing system used in the Sinosphere region</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the moth known as the "Chinese character", see <a href="/wiki/Cilix_glaucata" title="Cilix glaucata"><i>Cilix glaucata</i></a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Unless otherwise specified, Chinese text in this article is presented as <i><a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a> or <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a></i>. If the simplified and traditional characters are the same, they are written only once.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="padding:0.25em; background:mistyrose;">Chinese characters<br/><div style="font-weight:normal"></div></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.6em;">Script type</th><td class="infobox-data"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;line-height:1.25em"> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logographic" title="Logographic">Logographic</a>
</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.6em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Time period</div></th><td class="infobox-data">13th century BC to present</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.6em;">Direction</th><td class="infobox-data">Left-to-right (modern)<br/>Top-to-bottom, columns right-to-left (<a href="/wiki/Horizontal_and_vertical_writing_in_East_Asian_scripts" title="Horizontal and vertical writing in East Asian scripts">traditional</a>)</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.6em;">Languages</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhuang_languages" title="Zhuang languages">Zhuang</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miao_languages" title="Miao languages">Miao</a>, etc</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background:#ddd">Related scripts</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.6em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Parent systems</div></th><td class="infobox-data"><div style="padding-top:0.15em;"><a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">Oracle bone script</a><ul><li>Chinese characters</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background:#ddd">ISO 15924</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.6em;"><a href="/wiki/ISO_15924" title="ISO 15924">ISO 15924</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886049734">.mw-parser-output .monospaced{font-family:monospace,monospace}</style><span class="monospaced">Hani</span> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886049734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="monospaced">(500)</span>, ​Han (Hanzi, Kanji, Hanja)</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background:#ddd"><a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="padding-right:0.6em;"><div style="display: inline-block; For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>. </tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="border-top:1px solid #c9b6a3;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Chinese_characters_sidebar" title="Template:Chinese characters sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Chinese_characters_sidebar" title="Template talk:Chinese characters sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Chinese_characters_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Chinese characters</b> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">漢字</span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans">汉字</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin"><b>hànzì</b></span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">han<sup>4</sup> tzŭ<sup>4</sup></span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Jyutping" title="Jyutping">Jyutping</a>: <i><span lang="yue-Latn-jyutping"><b>hon3 zi6</b></span></i>; <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a></small> '<a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han</a> characters') are <a href="/wiki/Logogram" title="Logogram">logograms</a> developed for the <a href="/wiki/Written_Chinese" title="Written Chinese">writing of Chinese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> In addition, they have been adapted to write other East Asian languages, and remain a key component of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_writing_system" title="Japanese writing system">Japanese writing system</a> where they are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a></i>. Chinese characters in South Korea, which are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a></i>, retain significant use in Korean academia to study its documents, history, literature and records. Vietnam once used the <i><a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_H%C3%A1n" title="Chữ Hán">chữ Hán</a></i> and developed <a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_N%C3%B4m" title="Chữ Nôm">chữ Nôm</a> to write <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a> before turning to a <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_alphabet" title="Vietnamese alphabet">romanized alphabet</a>. Chinese characters are the oldest continuously used system of writing in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> By virtue of their widespread current use throughout <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, as well as their profound historic use throughout the <a href="/wiki/Adoption_of_Chinese_literary_culture" title="Adoption of Chinese literary culture">Sinosphere</a>, Chinese characters are among the most widely adopted writing systems in the world by number of users.
</p><p>The total number of Chinese characters ever to appear in a dictionary is in the tens of thousands, though most are graphic variants, or were used historically and passed out of use, or are of a specialized nature. A college graduate who is <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literate</a> in written Chinese knows between three and four thousand characters, though more are required for specialized fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198873_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198873-5">[5]</a></sup> In Japan, 2,136 are taught through secondary school (the <i><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">Jōyō kanji</a></i>); hundreds more are in everyday use. Due to separate simplifications of characters <a href="/wiki/Shinjitai" title="Shinjitai">in Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">in China</a>, the <i>kanji</i> used in Japan today has some differences from Chinese simplified characters in several respects. There are various national standard lists of characters, forms, and pronunciations. <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Simplified forms of certain characters</a> are used in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional characters</a> are used in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>, and, to some extent, in <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, common characters are often written in <a href="/wiki/Shinjitai" title="Shinjitai">post-Tōyō kanji simplified forms</a>, while uncommon characters are written in <a href="/wiki/Ky%C5%ABjitai" title="Kyūjitai">Japanese traditional forms</a>. During the 1970s, Singapore had also briefly enacted its <a href="/wiki/Singapore_Chinese_characters" title="Singapore Chinese characters">own simplification campaign</a>, but eventually streamlined its simplification to be uniform with mainland China.
</p><p>In modern Chinese, most words are <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compounds</a> written with two or more characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Unlike alphabetic writing systems, in which the unit character roughly corresponds to one <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phoneme</a>, the Chinese writing system associates each logogram with an entire <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllable</a>, and thus may be compared in some aspects to a <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabary</a>. A character almost always corresponds to a single syllable that is also a <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>  However, there are a few exceptions to this general correspondence, including bisyllabic morphemes (written with two characters), bimorphemic syllables (written with two characters) and cases where a single character represents a polysyllabic word or phrase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vmair_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-vmair-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Modern Chinese has many <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">homophones</a>; thus the same spoken syllable may be represented by one of many characters, depending on meaning. A particular character may also have a range of meanings, or sometimes quite distinct meanings, which might have different pronunciations. <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">Cognates</a> in the several <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a> are generally written with the same character. In other languages, most significantly in modern Japanese and sometimes in Korean, characters are used to represent Chinese <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a> or to represent native words independent of the Chinese pronunciation (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kun-yomi" title="Kun-yomi">kun'yomi</a> in Japanese). Some characters retained their phonetic elements based on their pronunciation in a historical variety of Chinese from which they were acquired. These foreign adaptations of Chinese pronunciation are known as <a href="/wiki/Sino-Xenic_pronunciations" title="Sino-Xenic pronunciations">Sino-Xenic pronunciations</a> and have been useful in the <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_reconstruction" title="Linguistic reconstruction">reconstruction</a> of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a>.
</p>

<a href="/wiki/Lists_of_ISO_639_codes" title="Lists of ISO 639 codes">ISO 639 code</a>. Wikipedia's <a href="/wiki/Category:Wikipedia_multilingual_support_templates" title="Category:Wikipedia multilingual support templates">multilingual support templates</a> may also be used.<span class="hide-when-compact"> <a href="/wiki/Template:Lang#Rationale" title="Template:Lang">See why</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2021</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>When the script was first used in the late 2nd millennium BC, words of <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">Old Chinese</a> were generally monosyllabic, and each character denoted a single word.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198858_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198858-9">[9]</a></sup> Increasing numbers of polysyllabic words have entered the language from the <a href="/wiki/Western_Zhou" title="Western Zhou">Western Zhou</a> period to the present day. It is estimated that about 25–30% of the vocabulary of classic texts from the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> was polysyllabic, though these words were used far less commonly than monosyllables, which accounted for 80–90% of occurrences in these texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilkinson201222_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilkinson201222-10">[10]</a></sup>
The process has accelerated over the centuries as phonetic change has increased the number of homophones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988112_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988112-11">[11]</a></sup>
It has been estimated that over two thirds of the 3,000 most common words in modern <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> are polysyllables, the vast majority of those being disyllables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYip200018_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYip200018-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common process has been to form <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compounds</a> of existing words, written with the characters of the constituent words. Words have also been created by adding <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affixes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reduplication" title="Reduplication">reduplication</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">borrowing</a> from other languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988155–156_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988155–156-13">[13]</a></sup>
Polysyllabic words are generally written with one character per syllable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198874_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198874-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[a]</a></sup>
In most cases the character denotes a <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morpheme</a> descended from an Old Chinese word.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198874–75_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198874–75-16">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many characters have multiple readings, with instances denoting different morphemes, sometimes with different pronunciations. In modern Standard Chinese, one fifth of the 2,400 most common characters have multiple pronunciations.
For the 500 most common characters, the proportion rises to 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>
Often these readings are similar in sound and related in meaning. In the Old Chinese period, <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affixes</a> could be added to a word to form a new word, which was often written with the same character. In many cases the pronunciations diverged due to subsequent <a href="/wiki/Sound_change" title="Sound change">sound change</a>. For example, many additional readings have the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> departing tone, the major source of the 4th tone in modern Standard Chinese. Scholars now believe that this tone is the reflex of an Old Chinese *-s suffix, with a range of semantic functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315–317_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315–317-18">[17]</a></sup>
For example,
</p>
<ul><li><span lang="zh-Hans">传</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">傳</span> has readings OC *<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">drjon</span></span> &gt; MC <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">drjwen'</i></span> &gt; Mod. <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">chuán</i></span> 'to transmit' and *<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">drjons</span></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">drjwenH</i></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">zhuàn</i></span> 'a record'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315-19">[18]</a></sup> (Middle Chinese forms are given in <a href="/wiki/Baxter%27s_transcription_for_Middle_Chinese" title="Baxter's transcription for Middle Chinese">Baxter's transcription</a>, in which <i>H</i> denotes the departing tone.)</li>
<li><span lang="zh">磨</span> has readings *maj &gt; <i>ma</i> &gt; <i>mó</i> 'to grind' and *majs &gt; <i>maH</i> &gt; <i>mò</i> 'grindstone'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992315-19">[18]</a></sup></li>
<li><span lang="zh">宿</span> has readings *<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">sjuk</span></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">sjuwk</i></span> &gt; <i>sù</i> 'to stay overnight' and *<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">sjuks</span></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">sjuwH</i></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">xiù</i></span> 'celestial "mansion"'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992316_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992316-20">[19]</a></sup></li>
<li><span lang="zh-Hans">说</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">説</span> has readings *<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">hljot</span></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">sywet</i></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">shuō</i></span> 'speak' and *<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">hljots</span></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">sywejH</i></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">shuì</i></span> 'exhort'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992197,_305_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992197,_305-21">[20]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Another common alternation is between voiced and voiceless initials (though the voicing distinction has disappeared on most modern varieties).
This is believed to reflect an ancient prefix, but scholars disagree on whether the voiced or voiceless form is the original root.
For example,
</p>
<ul><li><span lang="zh-Hans">见</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">見</span> has readings *kens &gt; <i>kenH</i> &gt; <i>jiàn</i> 'to see' and *gens &gt; <i>henH</i> &gt; <i>xiàn</i> 'to appear'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992218_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992218-22">[21]</a></sup></li>
<li><span lang="zh-Hans">败</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">敗</span> has readings *prats &gt; <i>pæjH</i> &gt; <i>bài</i> 'to defeat' and *brats &gt; <i>bæjH</i> &gt; <i>bài</i> 'to be defeated'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992218_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992218-22">[21]</a></sup> (In this case the pronunciations have converged in Standard Chinese, but not in some other varieties.)</li>
<li><span lang="zh">折</span> has readings *<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">tjat</i></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">tsyet</i></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">zhé</i></span> 'to bend' and *<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">djat</span></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">dzyet</i></span> &gt; <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">shé</i></span> 'to break by bending'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992219_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992219-23">[22]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Principles_of_formation">Principles of formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Principles of formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chineseprimer3.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="225" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Chineseprimer3.png/150px-Chineseprimer3.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1b/Chineseprimer3.png 1.5x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chineseprimer3.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Excerpt from a 1436 primer on Chinese characters</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_character_classification" title="Chinese character classification">Chinese character classification</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evo-r%C3%AC.svg"><img alt="" class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evo-sh%C4%81n.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="150" data-file-width="500" class="tsingle" style="width:302px;max-width:302px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evo-elephant.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="321" data-file-width="998" 2x" width="300"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Elephant</div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Evolution of pictograms</div></div></div></div>
<p>Chinese characters represent words of the language using several strategies. A few characters, including some of the most commonly used, were originally <a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">pictograms</a>, which depicted the objects denoted, or <a href="/wiki/Ideogram" title="Ideogram">ideograms</a>, in which meaning was expressed <a href="/wiki/Iconicity" title="Iconicity">iconically</a>. The vast majority were written using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rebus_principle" title="Rebus principle">rebus principle</a>, in which a character for a similarly sounding word was either simply borrowed or (more commonly) extended with a disambiguating semantic marker to form a <a href="#Phono-semantic_compounds">phono-semantic compound</a> character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198858–61_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198858–61-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The traditional six-fold classification (<i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">liùshū</i></span></i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">六书</span></span> / <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">六書</span></span> "six writings") was first described by the scholar <a href="/wiki/Xu_Shen" title="Xu Shen">Xu Shen</a> in the postface of his dictionary <i><a href="/wiki/Shuowen_Jiezi" title="Shuowen Jiezi">Shuowen Jiezi</a></i> in 100 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198867–69_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198867–69-25">[24]</a></sup>
While this analysis is sometimes problematic and arguably fails to reflect the complete nature of the Chinese writing system, it has been perpetuated by its long history and pervasive use.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pictograms">Pictograms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pictograms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">象形字</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">xiàngxíngzì</i></span></i></li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">Pictograms</a> are highly stylized and simplified pictures of material objects.  Examples of pictograms include <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">日</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">rì</i></span></i> for "sun", <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">月</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">yuè</i></span></i> for "moon", and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">木</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">mù</i></span></i> for "tree" or "wood". Xu Shen placed approximately 4% of characters in this category.
Though few in number and expressing literal objects, pictograms and ideograms are nonetheless the basis on which all the more complex characters such as associative compound characters (会意字/會意字) and phono-semantic characters (形声字/形聲字) are formed.
</p><p>Pictograms are primary characters in the sense that they, along with ideograms (indicative characters i.e. symbols), are the building blocks of associative compound characters (会意字/會意字) and phono-semantic characters (形声字/形聲字).
</p><p>Over time pictograms were increasingly standardized, simplified, and stylized to make them easier to write. Furthermore, the same <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_radical" title="Kangxi radical">Kangxi radical</a> character element can be used to depict different objects.
Thus, the image depicted by most pictograms is not often immediately evident. For example, 口 may indicate the mouth, a window as in 高 which depicts a tall building as a symbol of the idea of "tall" or the lip of a vessel as in 富 a wine jar under a roof as symbol of wealth.  That is, pictograms extended from literal objects to take on symbolic or metaphoric meanings; sometimes even displacing the use of the character as a literal term, or creating ambiguity, which was resolved through character determinants, more commonly but less accurately known as "<a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_characters)" title="Radical (Chinese characters)">radicals</a>" i.e. concept keys in the <a href="#Phono-semantic_compounds">phono-semantic</a> characters.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Simple_ideograms">Simple ideograms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Simple ideograms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">指事字</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zhǐshìzì</i></span></i></li></ul>
<p>Also called <i>simple indicatives,</i> this small category contains characters that are direct <a href="/wiki/Iconicity" title="Iconicity">iconic</a> illustrations. Examples include <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">上</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shàng</i></span></i> "up" and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">下</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">xià</i></span></i> "down", originally a dot above and below a line. Indicative characters are symbols for abstract concepts which could not be depicted literally but nonetheless can be expressed as a visual symbol e.g. convex 凸, concave 凹, flat-and-level 平.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compound_ideographs">Compound ideographs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Compound ideographs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">会意字</span></span> / <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">會意字</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">huìyìzì</i></span></i></li></ul>
<p>Also translated as logical aggregates or associative idea characters, these characters have been interpreted as combining two or more pictographic or ideographic characters to suggest a third meaning.  The canonical example is <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">明</span></span> bright. 明 is the association of the two brightest objects in the sky the sun 日 and moon 月, brought together to express the idea of "bright". It is canonical because the term 明白 in Chinese (lit. "bright white") means "to understand, understand". Adding the abbreviated radical for grass, cao <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">艹</span></span> above the character, ming, changes it to meng 萌, which means to sprout or bud, alluding to the heliotropic behavior of plant life. Other commonly cited examples include <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">休</span></span> "rest" (composed of the pictograms <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">人</span></span> "person" and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">木</span></span> "tree") and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">好</span></span> "good" (composed of <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">女</span></span> "woman" and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">子</span></span> "child").
</p><p>Xu Shen placed approximately 13% of characters in this category, but many of his examples are now believed to be <a href="#Phono-semantic_compounds">phono-semantic compounds</a> whose origin has been obscured by subsequent changes in their form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013261_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013261-26">[25]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Boodberg" title="Peter Boodberg">Peter Boodberg</a> and William Boltz go so far as to deny that any of the compound characters devised in ancient times were of this type, maintaining that now-lost "secondary readings" are responsible for the apparent absence of phonetic indicators,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoltz1994104–110_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoltz1994104–110-27">[26]</a></sup> but their arguments have been rejected by other scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013265–268_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013265–268-28">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>In contrast, associative compound characters are common among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kokuji" title="Kokuji">characters coined in Japan</a>. Also, a few characters coined in China in modern times, such as <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">鉑</span></span> platinum, "white metal" (see <i><a href="/wiki/Chemical_elements_in_East_Asian_languages" title="Chemical elements in East Asian languages">Chemical elements in East Asian languages</a></i>) belong to this category.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebus">Rebus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Rebus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">假借字</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">jiǎjièzì</i></span></i></li></ul>
<p>Also called <i>borrowings</i> or <i>phonetic loan characters,</i> the <a href="/wiki/Rebus" title="Rebus">rebus</a> category covers cases where an existing character is used to represent an unrelated word with similar or identical pronunciation; sometimes the old meaning is then lost completely, as with characters such as <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">自</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zì</i></span></i>, which has lost its original meaning of "nose" completely and exclusively means "oneself", or <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">萬</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">wàn</i></span></i>, which originally meant "scorpion" but is now used only in the sense of "ten thousand".
</p><p>Rebus was pivotal in the history of writing in China insofar as it represented the stage at which logographic writing could become purely phonetic (phonographic). Chinese characters used purely for their sound values are attested in the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> manuscripts, in which <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zhi</i></span></i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">氏</span></span> was used to write <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shi</i></span></i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">是</span></span> and vice versa, just lines apart; the same happened with <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">shao</i></span></i> 勺 for <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Zhao</i></span></i> <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">趙</span></span>, with the characters in question being homophonous or nearly homophonous at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoltz1994169_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoltz1994169-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonetical_usage_for_foreign_words">Phonetical usage for foreign words</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Phonetical usage for foreign words">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Chinese characters are used rebus-like and exclusively for their phonetic value when transcribing words of foreign origin, such as ancient Buddhist terms or modern foreign names. For example, the word for the country "<a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>" is 罗马尼亚/羅馬尼亞 (Luó Mǎ Ní Yà), in which the Chinese characters are only used for their sounds and do not provide any meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AEG_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-AEG-30">[29]</a></sup> This usage is similar to that of the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">Katakana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">Hiragana</a>, although the Kanas use a special set of simplified forms of Chinese characters, in order to advertise their value as purely phonetic symbols. The same rebus principle for names in particular has also been used in <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyphs" title="Egyptian hieroglyphs">Egyptian hieroglyphs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maya_glyphs" title="Maya glyphs">Maya glyphs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DW_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-DW-31">[30]</a></sup> In the Chinese usage, in a few instances, the characters used for pronunciation might be carefully chosen in order to connote a specific meaning, as regularly happens for brand names: <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> is translated phonetically as 可口可乐/可口可樂 (Kěkǒu Kělè), but the characters were carefully selected so as to have the additional meaning of "Delicious and Enjoyable". A more literal translation would be "the Mouth can be happy", and the phrase in Chinese is technically grammatically sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AEG_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-AEG-30">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DW_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-DW-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phono-semantic_compounds">Phono-semantic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Phono-semantic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%BD%A2%E5%A3%B0%E5%AD%97" title="wikt:形声字">形声字</a></span></span> / <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%BD%A2%E8%81%B2%E5%AD%97" title="wikt:形聲字">形聲字</a></span></span> Mandarin: <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">xíngshēngzì</i></span></i></li></ul>

class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Busyu-kamae6.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="8" data-file-width="8" decoding="async" height="30" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Busyu-kamae6.svg/45px-Busyu-kamae6.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Busyu-kamae6.svg/60px-Busyu-kamae6.svg.png 2x" width="30"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Structures of compounds, with red marked positions of <a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_characters)" title="Radical (Chinese characters)">radicals</a></div></div></div></div>
<p><i>Semantic-phonetic compounds</i> or <i>pictophonetic compounds</i> are by far the most numerous characters. These characters are composed of at least two parts. The <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantic</a> component suggests the general meaning of the compound character. The <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">phonetic</a> component suggests the pronunciation of the compound character. In most cases the semantic indicator is also the 部首 <a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_characters)" title="Radical (Chinese characters)">radical</a> under which the character is listed in dictionaries. In some rare examples phono-semantic characters may also convey pictorial content. Each Chinese character is an attempt to combine sound, image, and idea in a mutually reinforcing fashion.
</p><p>Examples of phono-semantic characters include <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">河</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">hé</i></span></i> "river", <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">湖</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">hú</i></span></i> "lake", <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">流</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">liú</i></span></i> "stream", <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">沖</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">chōng</i></span></i> "surge", <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">滑</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">huá</i></span></i> "slippery". All these characters have on the left a radical of three short strokes (氵), which is a reduced form of the character 水 <i>shuǐ</i> meaning "water", indicating that the character has a semantic connection with water. The right-hand side in each case is a phonetic indicator- for instance: <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">胡</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">hú</i></span></i> has a very similar pronunciation to <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">湖</span></span> and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">可</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">kě</i></span></i> has a similar (though somewhat different) pronunciation to 河. For example, in the case of <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">沖</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">chōng</i></span></i> (Old Chinese <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*ɡ-ljuŋ</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992750_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992750-32">[31]</a></sup>) "surge", the phonetic indicator is <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">中</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zhōng</i></span></i> (Old Chinese <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*k-ljuŋ</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992810_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992810-33">[32]</a></sup>), which by itself means "middle". In this case it can be seen that the pronunciation of the character is slightly different from that of its phonetic indicator; the effect of historical sound change means that the composition of such characters can sometimes seem arbitrary today.
</p><p>In general, phonetic components do not determine the exact pronunciation of a character, but only give a clue as to its pronunciation. While some characters take the exact pronunciation of their phonetic component, others take only the initial or final sounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliams_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliams-34">[33]</a></sup> In fact, some characters' pronunciations may not correspond to the pronunciations of their phonetic parts at all, which is sometimes the case with characters after having undergone simplification. The 8 characters in the following table all take 也 for their phonetic part, however, as it is readily apparent, none of them take the pronunciation of 也, which is yě (Old Chinese *lajʔ).  As the table below shows, the sound changes that have taken place since the Shang/Zhou period when most of these characters were created can be dramatic, to the point of not providing any useful hint of the modern pronunciation.
</p>

<p>Xu Shen (c. 100 AD) placed approximately 82% of characters into this category, while in the <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Dictionary" title="Kangxi Dictionary">Kangxi Dictionary</a> (1716 AD) the number is closer to 90%, due to the extremely productive use of this technique to extend the Chinese vocabulary.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_N%C3%B4m" title="Chữ Nôm">chữ Nôm</a> characters of Vietnam were created using this principle.
</p><p>This method is used to form new characters, for example <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">钚</span></span> / <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">鈈</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">bù</i></span></i> ("<a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a>") is the metal radical <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">金</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">jīn</i></span></i> plus the phonetic component <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">不</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">bù</i></span></i>, described in Chinese as "<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">不</span></span> gives sound, <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">金</span></span> gives meaning". Many <a href="/wiki/Chemical_elements_in_East_Asian_languages" title="Chemical elements in East Asian languages">Chinese names of the chemical elements</a> and many other <a href="/wiki/Organic_nomenclature_in_Chinese" title="Organic nomenclature in Chinese">chemistry-related characters</a> were formed this way. In fact, it is possible to tell from a Chinese periodic table at a glance which elements are metal (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">金</span></span>), solid nonmetal (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">石</span></span>, "stone"), liquid (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">氵</span></span>), or gas (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">气</span></span>) at <a href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>.
</p><p>Occasionally a bisyllabic word is written with two characters that contain the same radical, as in <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">蝴蝶</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">húdié</i></span></i> "butterfly", where both characters have the insect radical <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">虫</span></span>. A notable example is <a href="/wiki/Pipa" title="Pipa">pipa</a> (a Chinese lute, also a fruit, the <a href="/wiki/Loquat" title="Loquat">loquat</a>, of similar shape) – originally written as <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">批把</span></span> with the hand radical (扌), referring to the down and up strokes when playing this instrument, which was then changed to <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">枇杷</span></span> (tree radical <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">木</span></span>), which is still used for the fruit, while the character was changed to <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">琵琶</span></span> when referring to the instrument (radical <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">玨</span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> In other cases a compound word may coincidentally share a radical without this being meaningful.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Derivative_cognates">Derivative cognates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Derivative cognates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hans">转注字</span></span> / <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">轉注字</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zhuǎnzhùzì</i></span></i></li></ul>
<p>The smallest category of characters is also the least understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198869_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198869-38">[37]</a></sup> In the postface to the <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Shuowen Jiezi</i></span></i>, Xu Shen gave as an example the characters <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">考</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">kǎo</i></span></i> "to verify" and <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">老</span></span> <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">lǎo</i></span></i> "old", which had similar Old Chinese pronunciations (*khuʔ and *C-ruʔ respectively<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992771,_772_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaxter1992771,_772-39">[38]</a></sup>) and may once have been the same word, meaning "elderly person", but became <a href="/wiki/Lexicalization" title="Lexicalization">lexicalized</a> into two separate words. The term does not appear in the body of the dictionary, and is often omitted from modern systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013260–261_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESampsonChen2013260–261-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform,_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1355" data-file-width="875" decoding="async" height="341" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg/220px-Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg/330px-Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg/440px-Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform%2C_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparative_evolution_of_Cuneiform,_Egyptian_and_Chinese_characters.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparative evolution from pictograms to abstract shapes, in cuneiform, Egyptian and Chinese characters</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legendary_origins">Legendary origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Legendary origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to traditional legend, Chinese characters were invented by <a href="/wiki/Cangjie" title="Cangjie">Cangjie</a>, a figure said to have been a scribe to the legendary <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Emperor" title="Yellow Emperor">Yellow Emperor</a> during the 27th century BC. Inspired by his study of the animals of the world, the landscape of the earth and the stars in the sky, Cangjie is said to have invented symbols called <i><span title="Chinese-language text"><i lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">zì</i></span></i> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">字</span></span>) – the first Chinese characters. The legend relates that on the day the characters were created, grain rained down from the sky and that night the people heard ghosts wailing and demons crying because the human beings could no longer be cheated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_sign_use">Early sign use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Early sign use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_signs_in_China" title="Neolithic signs in China">Neolithic signs in China</a></div>
<p>In recent decades, a series of inscribed graphs and pictures have been found at <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> sites in China, including <a href="/wiki/Jiahu" title="Jiahu">Jiahu</a> (c. 6500 BC), <a href="/wiki/Dadiwan_culture" title="Dadiwan culture">Dadiwan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damaidi" title="Damaidi">Damaidi</a> from the 6th millennium BC, and <a href="/wiki/Banpo" title="Banpo">Banpo</a> (5th millennium BC).  Often these finds are accompanied by media reports that push back the purported beginnings of Chinese writing by thousands of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincon_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-rincon-43">[42]</a></sup> However, because these marks occur singly, without any implied context, and are made crudely and simply, <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Xigui" title="Qiu Xigui">Qiu Xigui</a> concluded that "we do not have any basis for stating that these constituted writing nor is there reason to conclude that they were ancestral to <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> Chinese characters."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu200031_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu200031-44">[43]</a></sup> They do however demonstrate a history of sign use in the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> valley during the Neolithic through to the Shang period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincon_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-rincon-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oracle_bone_script">Oracle bone script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Oracle bone script">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">Oracle bone script</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2669" data-file-width="1733" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg/170px-Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg/255px-Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg/340px-Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shang_dynasty_inscribed_scapula.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ox scapula with oracle bone inscription</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest confirmed evidence of the Chinese script yet discovered is the body of inscriptions carved on bronze vessels and <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone" title="Oracle bone">oracle bones</a> from the late <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a> (c. 1250–1050 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20101_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20101-45">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeightley1978xvi_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeightley1978xvi-46">[45]</a></sup> The earliest of these is dated to around 1200 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bagley_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bagley-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boltz_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boltz-48">[47]</a></sup> In 1899, pieces of these bones were being sold as "dragon bones" for medicinal purposes, when scholars identified the symbols on them as Chinese writing. By 1928, the source of the bones had been traced to a village near <a href="/wiki/Anyang" title="Anyang">Anyang</a> in <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan Province</a>, which was excavated by the <a href="/wiki/Academia_Sinica" title="Academia Sinica">Academia Sinica</a> between 1928 and 1937. Over 150,000 fragments have been found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20101_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20101-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Oracle bone inscriptions are records of divinations performed in communication with royal ancestral spirits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20101_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20101-45">[44]</a></sup> The shortest are only a few characters long, while the longest are 30 to 40 characters in length. The Shang king would communicate with his ancestors on topics relating to the royal family, military success, weather forecasting, ritual sacrifices, and related topics by means of <a href="/wiki/Scapulimancy" title="Scapulimancy">scapulimancy</a>, and the answers would be recorded on the divination material itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20101_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20101-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">oracle bone script</a> is a well-developed writing system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoltz1986424_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoltz1986424-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeightley1996_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeightley1996-50">[49]</a></sup> suggesting that the Chinese script's origins may lie earlier than the late second millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20102_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20102-51">[50]</a></sup>  Although these divinatory inscriptions are the earliest surviving evidence of ancient Chinese writing, it is widely believed that writing was used for many other non-official purposes, but that the materials upon which non-divinatory writing was done – likely wood and bamboo – were less durable than bone and shell and have since decayed away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKern20102_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKern20102-51">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age:_parallel_script_forms_and_gradual_evolution">Bronze Age: parallel script forms and gradual evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Bronze Age: parallel script forms and gradual evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shi_Qiang_pan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="603" data-file-width="804" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Shi_Qiang_pan.jpg/220px-Shi_Qiang_pan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Shi_Qiang_pan.jpg/330px-Shi_Qiang_pan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Shi_Qiang_pan.jpg/440px-Shi_Qiang_pan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shi_Qiang_pan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Shi_Qiang_pan" title="Shi Qiang pan">Shi Qiang pan</a>, a bronze ritual basin dated to around 900 BC. Long inscriptions on the surface describe the deeds and virtues of the first seven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhou_Dynasty" title="Zhou Dynasty">Zhou</a> kings.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bronze_inscriptions" title="Chinese bronze inscriptions">Chinese bronze inscriptions</a></div>
<p>The traditional picture of an orderly series of scripts, each one invented suddenly and then completely displacing the previous one, has been conclusively demonstrated to be fiction by the archaeological finds and scholarly research of the later 20th and early 21st centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu200063–64,_66,_86,_88–89,_104–107,_124_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu200063–64,_66,_86,_88–89,_104–107,_124-52">[51]</a></sup> Gradual evolution and the coexistence of two or more scripts was more often the case. As early as the Shang dynasty, oracle bone script coexisted as a simplified form alongside the normal script of <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a> books (preserved in typical <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bronze_inscriptions" title="Chinese bronze inscriptions">bronze inscriptions</a>), as well as the extra-elaborate pictorial forms (often clan emblems) found on many bronzes.
</p><p>Based on studies of these bronze inscriptions, it is clear that, from the Shang dynasty writing to that of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Zhou" title="Western Zhou">Western Zhou</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Zhou" title="Eastern Zhou">Eastern Zhou</a>, the mainstream script evolved in a slow, unbroken fashion, until assuming the form that is now known as <a href="/wiki/Seal_script" title="Seal script">seal script</a> in the late Eastern Zhou in the state of <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Qin</a>, without any clear line of division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu200059–150_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu200059–150-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Meanwhile, other scripts had evolved, especially in the eastern and southern areas during the late <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a>, including regional forms, such as the <i>gǔwén</i> ("ancient forms") of the eastern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warring_States" title="Warring States">Warring States</a> preserved as variant forms in the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> character dictionary <i><a href="/wiki/Shuowen_Jiezi" title="Shuowen Jiezi">Shuowen Jiezi</a></i>, as well as decorative forms such as <a href="/wiki/Bird-worm_seal_script" title="Bird-worm seal script">bird and insect scripts</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Unification:_seal_script.2C_vulgar_writing.2C_and_proto-clerical"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Unification:_seal_script,_vulgar_writing,_and_proto-clerical">Unification: seal script, vulgar writing, and proto-clerical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Unification: seal script, vulgar writing, and proto-clerical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar nomobile nowraplinks" style="width:auto;"><tbody><tr><th class="sidebar-title"><a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">Calligraphy</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content plainlist" style="padding-left:1.5em;padding-right:1.5em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Arabic_calligraphy" title="Arabic calligraphy">Arabic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chinese_calligraphy" title="Chinese calligraphy">Chinese</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Georgian_calligraphy" title="Georgian calligraphy">Georgian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Indian_calligraphy" title="Indian calligraphy">Indian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Islamic_calligraphy" title="Islamic calligraphy">Islamic</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Japanese_calligraphy" title="Japanese calligraphy">Japanese</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Korean_calligraphy" title="Korean calligraphy">Korean</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mongolian_calligraphy" title="Mongolian calligraphy">Mongolian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Persian_calligraphy" title="Persian calligraphy">Persian</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tibetan_calligraphy" title="Tibetan calligraphy">Tibetan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_calligraphy" title="Vietnamese calligraphy">Vietnamese</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Western_calligraphy" title="Western calligraphy">Western</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Calligraphy" title="Template:Calligraphy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Calligraphy" title="Template talk:Calligraphy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Calligraphy&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Seal_script" title="Seal script">Seal script</a>, which had evolved slowly in the state of Qin during the Eastern <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a>, became standardized and adopted as the formal script for all of China in the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> (leading to a popular misconception that it was invented at that time), and was still widely used for decorative engraving and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seal_(device)" title="Seal (device)">seals</a> (name chops, or signets) in the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> period. However, despite the Qin script standardization, more than one script remained in use at the time. For example, a little-known, rectilinear and roughly executed kind of common (vulgar) writing had for centuries coexisted with the more formal seal script in the <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Qin state</a>, and the popularity of this vulgar writing grew as the use of writing itself became more widespread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000104_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000104-55">[54]</a></sup> By the <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a>, an immature form of <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">clerical script</a> called "early clerical" or "proto-clerical" had already developed in the state of Qin<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu200059,_104–107_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu200059,_104–107-56">[55]</a></sup> based upon this vulgar writing, and with influence from seal script as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000119_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000119-57">[56]</a></sup> The coexistence of the three scripts – small seal, vulgar and proto-clerical, with the latter evolving gradually in the Qin to early Han dynasties into <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">clerical script</a> – runs counter to the traditional belief that the Qin dynasty had one script only, and that clerical script was suddenly invented in the early Han dynasty from the <a href="/wiki/Small_seal_script" title="Small seal script">small seal script</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Han_dynasty">Han dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Han dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proto-clerical_evolving_to_clerical">Proto-clerical evolving to clerical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Proto-clerical evolving to clerical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Proto-clerical script, which had emerged by the time of the Warring States period from vulgar Qin writing, matured gradually, and by the early Western Han period, it was little different from that of the Qin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000123_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000123-58">[57]</a></sup> Recently discovered bamboo slips show the script becoming mature clerical script by the middle-to-late reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu of the Western Han</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000119,_123–124_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000119,_123–124-59">[58]</a></sup> who ruled from 141 to 87 BC.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Clerical_and_clerical_cursive">Clerical and clerical cursive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Clerical and clerical cursive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Contrary to the popular belief of there being only one script per period, there were in fact multiple scripts in use during the Han period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000130_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000130-60">[59]</a></sup> Although mature <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">clerical script</a>, also called <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">八分</span></span> (<span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">bāfēn</span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000121_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000121-61">[60]</a></sup> script, was dominant at that time, an early type of <a href="/wiki/Cursive_script_(East_Asia)" title="Cursive script (East Asia)">cursive script</a> was also in use by the Han by at least as early as 24 BC (during the very late Western Han period),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[b]</a></sup> incorporating cursive forms popular at the time, well as many elements from the vulgar writing of the <a href="/wiki/Qin_(state)" title="Qin (state)">Warring State of Qin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000131,_133_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000131,_133-63">[61]</a></sup> By around the time of the Eastern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin dynasty (265–420)">Jin dynasty</a>, this Han cursive became known as <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">章草</span></span> <i>zhāngcǎo</i> (also known as <span lang="zh-Hans">隶草</span> / <span lang="zh-Hant">隸草</span> <i>lìcǎo</i> today), or in English sometimes clerical cursive, ancient cursive, or draft cursive. Some believe that the name, based on <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">章</span></span> <i>zhāng</i> meaning "orderly", arose because the script was a more orderly form<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138-64">[62]</a></sup> of cursive than <a href="/wiki/Cursive_script_(East_Asia)" title="Cursive script (East Asia)">the modern form</a>, which emerged during the Eastern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin dynasty (265–420)">Jin dynasty</a> and is still in use today, called <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">今草</span></span> <i>jīncǎo</i> or "modern cursive".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000131_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000131-65">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-clerical">Neo-clerical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Neo-clerical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Around the mid-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han" title="Eastern Han">Eastern Han</a> period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138-64">[62]</a></sup> a simplified and easier-to-write form of clerical script appeared, which Qiu terms "neo-clerical" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">新隶体</span></span> / <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">新隸體</span></span>, <i>xīnlìtǐ</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000113,_139_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000113,_139-66">[64]</a></sup> By the late Eastern Han, this had become the dominant daily script,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138-64">[62]</a></sup> although the formal, mature <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">bāfēn</i></span> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">八分</span></span>) clerical script remained in use for formal works such as engraved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stelae" title="Stelae">stelae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138-64">[62]</a></sup> Qiu describes this neo-clerical script as a transition between clerical and regular script,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138_64-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000138-64">[62]</a></sup> and it remained in use through the <a href="/wiki/Cao_Wei" title="Cao Wei">Cao Wei</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin dynasty (265–420)">Jin dynasties</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000139_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000139-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Semi-cursive">Semi-cursive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Semi-cursive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>By the late Eastern Han period, an early form of <a href="/wiki/Semi-cursive_script" title="Semi-cursive script">semi-cursive script</a> appeared,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000113,_139_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000113,_139-66">[64]</a></sup> developing out of a cursively written form of neo-clerical script<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[c]</a></sup> and simple cursive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000142_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000142-69">[66]</a></sup> This semi-cursive script was traditionally attributed to Liu Desheng c. 147–188 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000139_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000139-67">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[d]</a></sup> although such attributions refer to early masters of a script rather than to their actual inventors, since the scripts generally evolved into being over time. Qiu gives examples of early semi-cursive script, showing that it had popular origins rather than being purely Liu's invention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000140_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000140-71">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wei_to_Jin_period">Wei to Jin period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Wei to Jin period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Regular_script">Regular script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Regular script">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
