<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="636" data-file-width="425" decoding="async" height="329" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg/220px-%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg/330px-%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E6%B5%99%E6%B1%9F%E5%A7%93%E8%A7%A31.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page from a <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> publication in a <a href="/wiki/Regular_script" title="Regular script">regular script</a> typeface which resembles the handwriting of <a href="/wiki/Ouyang_Xun" title="Ouyang Xun">Ouyang Xun</a> from Tang Dynasty</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Regular_script" title="Regular script">Regular script</a> has been attributed to <a href="/wiki/Zhong_Yao" title="Zhong Yao">Zhong Yao</a> (c. 151–230 AD), during the period at the <a href="/wiki/End_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="End of the Han dynasty">end of the Han dynasty</a> in the state of <a href="/wiki/Cao_Wei" title="Cao Wei">Cao Wei</a>. Zhong Yao has been called the "father of regular script". However, some scholars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup> postulate that one person alone could not have developed a new script which was universally adopted, but could only have been a contributor to its gradual formation. The earliest surviving pieces written in regular script are copies of Zhong Yao's works, including at least one copied by <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xizhi" title="Wang Xizhi">Wang Xizhi</a>. This new script, which is the dominant modern Chinese script, developed out of a neatly written form of early semi-cursive, with addition of the pause (<span lang="zh-Hans">顿</span>/<span lang="zh-Hant">頓</span> <i>dùn</i>) technique to end horizontal strokes, plus heavy tails on strokes which are written to the downward-right diagonal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000143_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000143-73">[69]</a></sup> Thus, early regular script emerged from a neat, formal form of semi-cursive, which had itself emerged from neo-clerical (a simplified, convenient form of clerical script). It then matured further in the Eastern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin dynasty (265–420)">Jin dynasty</a> in the hands of the "Sage of Calligraphy", <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xizhi" title="Wang Xizhi">Wang Xizhi</a>, and his son <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xianzhi_(calligrapher)" title="Wang Xianzhi (calligrapher)">Wang Xianzhi</a>. It was not, however, in widespread use at that time, and most writers continued using neo-clerical, or a somewhat semi-cursive form of it, for daily writing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000143_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000143-73">[69]</a></sup> while the conservative <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">bafen</i></span> clerical script remained in use on some stelae, alongside some semi-cursive, but primarily neo-clerical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000144_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000144-74">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_cursive">Modern cursive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Modern cursive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Meanwhile, modern cursive script slowly emerged from the clerical cursive (<i>zhāngcǎo</i>) script during the Cao Wei to Jin period, under the influence of both semi-cursive and the newly emerged regular script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000148_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000148-75">[71]</a></sup> Cursive was formalized in the hands of a few master calligraphers, the most famous and influential of whom was <a href="/wiki/Wang_Xizhi" title="Wang Xizhi">Wang Xizhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[e]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dominance_and_maturation_of_regular_script">Dominance and maturation of regular script</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Dominance and maturation of regular script">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It was not until the <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">Northern and Southern dynasties</a> that regular script rose to dominant status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEQiu2000145_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEQiu2000145-77">[72]</a></sup> During that period, regular script continued evolving stylistically, reaching full maturity in the early <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>. Some call the writing of the early Tang calligrapher <a href="/wiki/Ouyang_Xun" title="Ouyang Xun">Ouyang Xun</a> (557–641) the first mature regular script. After this point, although developments in the art of calligraphy and in character simplification still lay ahead, there were no more major stages of evolution for the mainstream script.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Modern history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although most simplified Chinese characters in use today are the result of the works moderated by the government of the People's Republic of China (PRC) in the 1950s and 60s, the use of some of these forms predates the PRC's formation in 1949. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caoshu" title="Caoshu">Caoshu</a>, cursive written text, was the inspiration of some simplified characters, and for others, some are attested as early as the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> (221–206 BC) as either vulgar variants or original characters.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ROC24_SC1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="624" decoding="async" height="423" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/ROC24_SC1.jpg/220px-ROC24_SC1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/ROC24_SC1.jpg/330px-ROC24_SC1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/ROC24_SC1.jpg/440px-ROC24_SC1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ROC24_SC1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first batch of Simplified Characters introduced in 1935 consisted of 324 characters.</div></div></div>
<p>One of the earliest proponents of character simplification was <a href="/wiki/Lufei_Kui" title="Lufei Kui">Lufei Kui</a>, who proposed in 1909 that simplified characters should be used in education. In the years following the <a href="/wiki/May_Fourth_Movement" title="May Fourth Movement">May Fourth Movement</a> in 1919, many <a href="/wiki/Anti-imperialism" title="Anti-imperialism">anti-imperialist</a> Chinese intellectuals sought ways to modernise China as quickly as possible. Traditional culture and values such as <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> were challenged and subsequently blamed for their problems. Soon, people in the Movement started to cite the traditional Chinese writing system as an obstacle in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_China" title="Modern China">modernising China</a> and therefore proposed that a reform be initiated. It was suggested that the Chinese writing system should be either simplified or completely abolished. <a href="/wiki/Lu_Xun" title="Lu Xun">Lu Xun</a>, a renowned Chinese author in the 20th century, stated that, "If Chinese characters are not destroyed, then China will die" (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh-Hant">漢字不滅，中國必亡</span></span>). Recent commentators have claimed that Chinese characters were blamed for the economic problems in China during that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yen_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yen-78">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1930s and 1940s, discussions on character simplification took place within the <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Kuomintang</a> government, and a large number of the intelligentsia maintained that character simplification would help boost literacy in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup> In 1935, 324 simplified characters collected by <a href="/wiki/Qian_Xuantong" title="Qian Xuantong">Qian Xuantong</a> were officially introduced as the table of first batch of simplified characters, but they were suspended in 1936 due to fierce opposition within the party.
</p><p>The People's Republic of China issued its first round of official character simplifications in two documents, the first in 1956 and the second in 1964. In the 1950s and 1960s, while confusion about simplified characters was still rampant, transitional characters that mixed simplified parts with yet-to-be simplified parts of characters together appeared briefly, then disappeared.
</p><p>"<a href="/wiki/Han_unification" title="Han unification">Han unification</a>" was an effort by the authors of Unicode and the Universal Character Set to map multiple character sets of the so-called CJK languages (Chinese/Japanese/Korean) into a single set of unified characters and was completed for the purposes of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> in 1991 (Unicode 1.0).
</p><p>Apart from Chinese ones, Korean, Japanese and Vietnamese normative medium of record-keeping, written historical narratives and official communication are in adaptations and variations of Chinese script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptation_to_other_languages">Adaptation to other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Adaptation to other languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1269" data-file-width="1971" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png/220px-Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png/330px-Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png/440px-Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese-Characters-Distribution.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Current (dark and medium green) and former extension (light green) of the use of Chinese characters</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map-Chinese_Characters.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1269" data-file-width="1971" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Map-Chinese_Characters.png/220px-Map-Chinese_Characters.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Map-Chinese_Characters.png/330px-Map-Chinese_Characters.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Map-Chinese_Characters.png/440px-Map-Chinese_Characters.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map-Chinese_Characters.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries and regions using Chinese characters as a writing system:<br/> <span style="color:#006622">Dark Green</span>: Traditional Chinese used officially (Taiwan, Hong Kong, Macau)<br/> <span style="color:Green">Green</span>: Simplified Chinese used officially but traditional form is also used in publishing (Singapore, Malaysia)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yzzk_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-yzzk-81">[76]</a></sup><br/> <span style="color:#00EE00">Light Green</span>: Simplified Chinese used officially, traditional form in daily use is uncommon (China, <a href="/wiki/Kokang" title="Kokang">Kokang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wa_State" title="Wa State">Wa State</a> of Myanmar)<br/> <span style="color:#4dff88">Cyan</span>: Chinese characters are used in parallel with other scripts in respective native languages (South Korea, Japan)<br/> <span style="color:#ffff80">Yellow</span>: Chinese characters were once used officially, but this is now obsolete (<a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>)</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_family_of_scripts" title="Chinese family of scripts">Chinese family of scripts</a></div>
<p>The Chinese script spread to <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> together with <a href="/wiki/Korean_Buddhism" title="Korean Buddhism">Buddhism</a> from the 2nd century BC to 5th century AD (<a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[77]</a></sup> This was adopted for recording the Japanese language from the 5th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[f]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chinese characters were first used in Vietnam during the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_under_Chinese_rule" title="Vietnam under Chinese rule">millennium of Chinese rule</a> starting in 111 BC. They were used to write Classical Chinese and adapted around the 13th century to create the <a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_N%C3%B4m" title="Chữ Nôm">chữ Nôm</a> script to write Vietnamese.
</p><p>Currently, the only non-Chinese language outside of China that regularly uses the Chinese script is Japanese. Vietnam abandoned its use in the early 20th century in favour of a Latin-based script, and Korea in the late 20th century in favour of its homegrown <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">hangul</a> script. Since the education of Chinese characters is not mandatory in South Korea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[78]</a></sup> the usage of Chinese character is rapidly disappearing.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese">Japanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Japanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">Kanji</a></div>
<p>Chinese characters adapted to write <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> words are known as <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a>. Chinese words borrowed into Japanese could be written with Chinese characters, while native Japanese words could also be written using the character(s) for a Chinese word of similar meaning. Most kanji have both the native (and often multi-syllabic) Japanese pronunciation, known as kun'yomi, and the (mono-syllabic) Chinese-based pronunciation, known as on'yomi. For example, the native Japanese word <i>katana</i> is written as <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">刀</span></span></span> in kanji, which uses the native pronunciation since the word is native to Japanese, while the Chinese loanword <i>nihontō</i> (meaning "Japanese sword") is written as <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">日本刀</span></span></span>, which uses the Chinese-based pronunciation. While nowadays loanwords from non-Sinosphere languages are usually just written in <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a>, one of the two syllabary systems of Japanese, loanwords that were borrowed into Japanese before the Meiji Period were typically written with Chinese characters whose on'yomi had the same pronunciation as the loanword itself, words like <i>Amerika</i> (kanji: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">亜米利加</span></span></span>, katakana: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">アメリカ</span></span></span>, meaning: America), <i>karuta</i> (kanji: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">歌留多</span></span></span>, <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">加留多</span></span></span>, katakana: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">カルタ</span></span></span>, meaning: card, letter), and <i>tenpura</i> (kanji: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">天婦羅</span></span></span>, <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">天麩羅</span></span></span>, katakana: <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">テンプラ</span></span></span>, meaning: tempura), although the meanings of the characters used often had no relation to the words themselves. Only some of the old kanji spellings are in common use, like <i>kan</i> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">缶</span></span></span>, meaning: can). Kanji that are used to only represent the sounds of a word are called <a href="/wiki/Ateji" title="Ateji">ateji</a> (<span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">当て字</span></span></span>).
Because Chinese words have been borrowed from varying dialects at different times, a single character may have several on'yomi in Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991122–129_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991122–129-85">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Written Japanese also includes a pair of <a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabaries</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Kana" title="Kana">kana</a>, derived by simplifying Chinese characters selected to represent syllables of Japanese.
The syllabaries differ because they sometimes selected different characters for a syllable, and because they used different strategies to reduce these characters for easy writing: the angular <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> were obtained by selecting a part of each character, while <a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a> were derived from the cursive forms of whole characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991129–132_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991129–132-86">[80]</a></sup>
Modern Japanese writing uses a composite system, using kanji for <a href="/wiki/Word_stem" title="Word stem">word stems</a>, hiragana for inflectional endings and grammatical words, and katakana to transcribe non-Chinese loanwords as well as serve as a method to emphasize native words (similar to how italics are used in Latin-script languages).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991132–133_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991132–133-87">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean">Korean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Korean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a></div>
<p>Throughout most of Korean history as early as the <a href="/wiki/Gojoseon" title="Gojoseon">Gojoseon</a> period up until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseon_Dynasty" title="Joseon Dynasty">Joseon Dynasty</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_Chinese" title="Literary Chinese">Literary Chinese</a> was the dominant form of written communication. Although the Korean alphabet <a href="/wiki/Hangul" title="Hangul">hangul</a> was created in 1443, it did not come into widespread official use until the late 19th and early 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fischer_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-fischer-89">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even today, much of the vocabulary, especially in the realms of science and sociology, comes directly from Chinese. However, due to the lack of tones in Modern Standard Korean, as the words were imported from Chinese, many dissimilar characters and syllables took on identical pronunciations, and subsequently identical spelling in hangul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIto_&amp;_Kenstowicz2017_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIto_&amp;_Kenstowicz2017-90">[84]</a></sup> Chinese characters are sometimes used to this day for either clarification in a practical manner, or to give a distinguished appearance, as knowledge of Chinese characters is considered by many Koreans a high class attribute and an indispensable part of a classical education. It is also observed that the preference for Chinese characters is treated as being culturally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confucian" title="Confucian">Confucian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fischer_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-fischer-89">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Korea, <i><a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a></i> have become a politically contentious issue, with some Koreans urging a "purification" of the national language and culture by totally abandoning their use. These individuals encourage the exclusive use of the native hangul alphabet throughout Korean society and the end to character education in public schools. Other Koreans support the revival of Hanja in everyday usage, like in the 1970s and 80s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHannas199768–72_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHannas199768–72-91">[85]</a></sup> In South Korea, educational policy on characters has swung back and forth, often swayed by education ministers' personal opinions. At present, middle and high school students (grades 7 to 12) are taught 1,800 characters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHannas199768–72_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHannas199768–72-91">[85]</a></sup> albeit with the principal focus on recognition, with the aim of achieving newspaper literacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fischer_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-fischer-89">[83]</a></sup> Hanja retains its prominence, especially in Korean academia, as the vast majority of Korean documents, history, literature and records (such as the <a href="/wiki/Veritable_Records_of_the_Joseon_Dynasty" title="Veritable Records of the Joseon Dynasty">Veritable Records of the Joseon Dynasty</a>, among others) were written in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_Chinese" title="Literary Chinese">Literary Chinese</a> using <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">Hanja</a> as its primary script. Therefore, a good working knowledge of Chinese characters is still important for anyone who wishes to interpret and study older texts from Korea, or anyone who wishes to read scholarly texts in the humanities. Hanja is also useful for understanding the <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sino-Korean_vocabulary" title="Sino-Korean vocabulary">Sino-Korean vocabulary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAS_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-BAS-92">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a clear trend toward the exclusive use of hangul in day-to-day South Korean society. Hanja are still used to some extent, particularly in newspapers, weddings, place names and <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a> (although it is nowhere near the extent of kanji use in day-to-day Japanese society). Hanja is also extensively used in situations where ambiguity must be avoided, such as academic papers, high-level corporate reports, government documents, and newspapers; this is due to the large number of <a href="/wiki/Homonym" title="Homonym">homonyms</a> that have resulted from <a href="/wiki/Sino-Korean_vocabulary" title="Sino-Korean vocabulary">extensive borrowing</a> of Chinese words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup> Characters convey meaning visually, while alphabets convey guidance to pronunciation, which in turn hints at meaning. As an example, in Korean dictionaries, the phonetic entry for <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">기사</span></span> <i>gisa</i> yields more than 30 different entries. In the past, this ambiguity had been efficiently resolved by parenthetically displaying the associated hanja. While hanja is sometimes used for Sino-Korean vocabulary, native Korean words are rarely, if ever, written in hanja.
</p><p>When learning how to write hanja, students are taught to memorize the native Korean pronunciation for the hanja's meaning and the Sino-Korean pronunciations (the pronunciation based on the Chinese pronunciation of the characters) for each hanja respectively so that students know what the syllable and meaning is for a particular hanja. For example, the name for the hanja <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">水</span></span> is <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">물</span></span> <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">수</span></span> (mul-su) in which <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">물</span></span> (mul) is the native Korean pronunciation for "water", while <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">수</span></span> (su) is the Sino-Korean pronunciation of the character. The naming of hanja is similar to if "water" were named "water-aqua", "horse-equus", or "gold-aurum" based on a hybridization of both the English and the Latin names. Other examples include <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">사람</span></span> <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">인</span></span> (saram-in) for <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">人</span></span> "person/people", <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">큰</span></span> <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">대</span></span> (keun-dae) for <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">大</span></span> "big/large//great", <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">작을</span></span> <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">소</span></span> (jakeul-so) for <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">小</span></span> "small/little", <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">아래</span></span> <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">하</span></span> (arae-ha) for <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">下</span></span> "underneath/below/low", <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">아비</span></span> <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">부</span></span> (abi-bu) for <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">父</span></span> "father", and <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">나라이름</span></span> <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">한</span></span> (naraireum-han) for <span title="Korean-language text"><span lang="ko">韓</span></span> "Han/Korea".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Korea">North Korea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: North Korea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In North Korea, the <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a> system was once completely banned since June 1949 due to fears of collapsed containment of the country; during the 1950s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kim_Il_Sung" title="Kim Il Sung">Kim Il Sung</a> had condemned all sorts of foreign languages (even the then-newly proposed <a href="/wiki/New_Korean_Orthography" title="New Korean Orthography">New Korean Orthography</a>). The ban continued into the 21st century. However, a textbook for university history departments containing 3,323 distinct characters was published in 1971.  In the 1990s, school children were still expected to learn 2,000 characters (more than in South Korea or Japan).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHannas199768_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHannas199768-95">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>After <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kim_Jong_Il" title="Kim Jong Il">Kim Jong Il</a>, the second ruler of North Korea, died in December 2011, his successor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kim_Jong_Un" title="Kim Jong Un">Kim Jong Un</a> began mandating the use of Hanja as a source of definition for the Korean language. Currently, it is said that North Korea teaches around 3,000 Hanja characters to North Korean students, and in some cases, the characters appear within advertisements and newspapers. However, it is also said that the authorities implore students not to use the characters in public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup> Due to North Korea's strict isolationism, accurate reports about hanja use in North Korea are hard to obtain.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Okinawan">Okinawan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Okinawan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Okinawan_language" title="Okinawan language">Okinawan language</a></div>
<p>Chinese characters are thought to have been first introduced to the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a> in 1265 by a Japanese Buddhist monk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[91]</a></sup> After the Okinawan kingdoms became tributaries of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_China" title="Ming China">Ming China</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Kingdom" title="Ryukyu Kingdom">Ryukyu Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">Classical Chinese</a> was used in court documents, but <a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a> was mostly used for popular writing and poetry. After Ryukyu became a vassal of Japan's <a href="/wiki/Satsuma_Domain" title="Satsuma Domain">Satsuma Domain</a>, Chinese characters became more popular, as well as the use of <a href="/wiki/Kanbun" title="Kanbun">Kanbun</a>. In modern Okinawan, which is labeled as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_dialect" title="Japanese dialect">Japanese dialect</a> by the Japanese government, <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a> and hiragana are mostly used to write Okinawan, but Chinese characters are still used.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnamese">Vietnamese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Vietnamese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_N%C3%B4m" title="Chữ Nôm">Chữ Nôm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Literary_Chinese_in_Vietnam" title="Literary Chinese in Vietnam">Literary Chinese in Vietnam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing_in_Vietnam" title="History of writing in Vietnam">History of writing in Vietnam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tale_of_Kieu_parallel_text.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="860" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Tale_of_Kieu_parallel_text.svg/330px-Tale_of_Kieu_parallel_text.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Tale_of_Kieu_parallel_text.svg/495px-Tale_of_Kieu_parallel_text.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Tale_of_Kieu_parallel_text.svg/660px-Tale_of_Kieu_parallel_text.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tale_of_Kieu_parallel_text.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first two lines of the classic Vietnamese epic poem <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Kieu" title="The Tale of Kieu">The Tale of Kieu</a></i>, written in the Nôm script and the modern Vietnamese alphabet. Chinese characters representing Sino-Vietnamese words are shown in green, characters borrowed for similar-sounding native Vietnamese words in purple, and invented characters in brown.</div></div></div>
<p>In Vietnam, Chinese characters (called <i><a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_H%C3%A1n" title="Chữ Hán">Chữ Hán</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_Nho" title="Chữ Nho">chữ Nho</a></i>, or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H%C3%A1n_t%E1%BB%B1" title="Hán tự">Hán tự</a></i> in Vietnamese) are now limited to ceremonial uses, but they were once in widespread use. Until the early 20th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_Chinese" title="Literary Chinese">Literary Chinese</a> was used in Vietnam for all official and scholarly writing.
</p><p>The oldest writing Chinese materials found in Vietnam is an epigraphy dated 618, erected by local <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a> officials in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thanh_Hoa" title="Thanh Hoa">Thanh Hoa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup> Around the 13th century, a script called <span title="Vietnamese-language text"><i lang="vi">chữ Nôm</i></span> was developed to record folk literature in the Vietnamese language. Similar to Zhuang <a href="/wiki/Sawndip" title="Sawndip">Sawndip</a>, the Nom script (demotic script) and its characters formed by fusing phonetic and semantic values of Chinese characters that resemble Vietnamese syllables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kornicki_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kornicki-99">[93]</a></sup> This process resulted in a highly complex system that was never mastered by more than 5% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup> The oldest writing Vietnamese <a href="/wiki/Ch%E1%BB%AF_N%C3%B4m" title="Chữ Nôm">chữ Nôm</a> script written along with Chinese is a Buddhist inscription, dated 1209.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kornicki_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kornicki-99">[93]</a></sup> In total, about 20,000 Chinese and Vietnamese epigraphy rubbings throughout Indochina were collected by the <a href="/wiki/French_School_of_the_Far_East" title="French School of the Far East">École française d'Extrême-Orient</a> (EFEO) library in Hanoi before 1945.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest surviving extant manuscript in Vietnam is a late 15th-century bilingual Buddhist sutra <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ph%E1%BA%ADt_thuy%E1%BA%BFt_%C4%91%E1%BA%A1i_b%C3%A1o_ph%E1%BB%A5_m%E1%BA%ABu_%C3%A2n_tr%E1%BB%8Dng_kinh&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Phật thuyết đại báo phụ mẫu ân trọng kinh (page does not exist)">Phật thuyết đại báo phụ mẫu ân trọng kinh</a>,</i> which is currently kept by the EFEO. The manuscript features Chinese texts in larger characters, and Vietnamese translation in smaller characters in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Vietnamese" title="Old Vietnamese">Old Vietnamese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup> Every Sino-Vietnamese book in Vietnam after the <i>Phật thuyết</i> are dated either from 17th century to 20th century, and most are hand-written/copied works, only few are printed texts. The <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_H%C3%A1n-N%C3%B4m_Studies" title="Institute of Hán-Nôm Studies">Institute of Hán-Nôm Studies</a>'s library in Hanoi had collected and kept 4,808 Sino-Vietnamese manuscripts in total by 1987.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French colonization</a> in the late 19th and early 20th century, Literary Chinese fell out of use and <span title="Vietnamese-language text"><i lang="vi">chữ Nôm</i></span> was gradually replaced with the Latin-based <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_alphabet" title="Vietnamese alphabet">Vietnamese alphabet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991113–115_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoulmas1991113–115-104">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis1977_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeFrancis1977-105">[99]</a></sup> Currently this alphabet is the main script in Vietnam, but Chinese characters and <span title="Vietnamese-language text"><i lang="vi">chữ Nôm</i></span> are still used in some activities connected with Vietnamese traditional culture (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphy</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_languages">Other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Other languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several minority languages of south and southwest China were formerly written with scripts based on Hanzi but also including many locally created characters.
The most extensive is the <i><a href="/wiki/Sawndip" title="Sawndip">sawndip</a></i> script for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhuang_language" title="Zhuang language">Zhuang language</a> of <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a> which is still used to this day.
Other languages written with such scripts include <a href="/wiki/Hmongic_languages" title="Hmongic languages">Miao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mienic_languages" title="Mienic languages">Yao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bouyei_language#Ancient_Bouyei_script" title="Bouyei language">Bouyei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mulam_language" title="Mulam language">Mulam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kam_language" title="Kam language">Kam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bai_language" title="Bai language">Bai</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hani_language" title="Hani language">Hani</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhou_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhou-106">[100]</a></sup> All these languages are now officially written using Latin-based scripts, while Chinese characters are still used for the Mulam language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Zhou Youguang article does not mention Mulam (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Even today for Zhuang, according to survey, the traditional sawndip script has twice as many users as the official Latin script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>The foreign dynasties that ruled northern China between the 10th and 13th centuries developed scripts that were inspired by Hanzi but did not use them directly: the <a href="/wiki/Khitan_large_script" title="Khitan large script">Khitan large script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khitan_small_script" title="Khitan small script">Khitan small script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tangut_script" title="Tangut script">Tangut script</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_script" title="Jurchen script">Jurchen script</a>.
Other scripts in China that borrowed or adapted a few Chinese characters but are otherwise distinct include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geba_script" title="Geba script">Geba script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sui_script" title="Sui script">Sui script</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yi_script" title="Yi script">Yi script</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lisu_syllabary" title="Lisu syllabary">Lisu syllabary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhou_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zhou-106">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transcription_of_foreign_languages">Transcription of foreign languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Transcription of foreign languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transcription_into_Chinese_characters" title="Transcription into Chinese characters">Transcription into Chinese characters</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Secret_history.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="607" decoding="async" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Secret_history.jpg/220px-Secret_history.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Secret_history.jpg/330px-Secret_history.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Secret_history.jpg/440px-Secret_history.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Secret_history.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mongolian text from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_History_of_the_Mongols" title="The Secret History of the Mongols">The Secret History of the Mongols</a></i> in Chinese transcription, with a glossary on the right of each row</div></div></div>
<p>Along with <a href="/wiki/Persian_alphabet" title="Persian alphabet">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">Arabic</a>, Chinese characters were also used as a foreign script to write the <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian language</a>, where characters were used to phonetically transcribe Mongolian sounds. Most notably, the only surviving copies of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_History_of_the_Mongols" title="The Secret History of the Mongols">The Secret History of the Mongols</a></i> were written in such a manner; the Chinese characters <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">忙豁侖紐察 脫[卜]察安</span></span> (nowadays pronounced "Mánghuōlún niǔchá tuō[bo]cháān" in Chinese) is the rendering of <i>Mongγol-un niγuca tobčiyan</i>, the title in Mongolian.
</p><p>Hanzi was also used to phonetically transcribe the <a href="/wiki/Manchu_language" title="Manchu language">Manchu language</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>.
</p><p>According to the Rev. John Gulick: "The inhabitants of other Asiatic nations, who have had occasion to represent the words of their several languages by Chinese characters, have as a rule used unaspirated characters for the sounds, g, d, b. The Muslims from Arabia and Persia have followed this method ... The Mongols, Manchu, and Japanese also constantly select unaspirated characters to represent the sounds g, d, b, and j of their languages. These surrounding Asiatic nations, in writing Chinese words in their own alphabets, have uniformly used g, d, b, etc., to represent the unaspirated sounds."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Simplification">Simplification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Simplification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Simplified Chinese characters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_script_reform" title="Japanese script reform">Japanese script reform</a></div>
<p>Chinese character simplification is the overall reduction of the number of strokes in the <a href="/wiki/Regular_script" title="Regular script">regular script</a> of a set of Chinese characters.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The use of traditional Chinese characters versus simplified Chinese characters varies greatly, and can depend on both the local customs and the medium. Before the official reform, character simplifications were not officially sanctioned and generally adopted vulgar variants and idiosyncratic substitutions. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vulgar_variant" title="Vulgar variant">Orthodox variants</a> were mandatory in printed works, while the (unofficial) simplified characters would be used in everyday writing or quick notes. Since the 1950s, and especially with the publication of the 1964 list, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a> has officially adopted <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese characters</a> for use in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, Macau, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> (Taiwan) were not affected by the reform. There is no absolute rule for using either system, and often it is determined by what the target audience understands, as well as the upbringing of the writer.
</p><p>Although most often associated with the People's Republic of China, character simplification predates the 1949 communist victory. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caoshu" title="Caoshu">Caoshu</a>, cursive written text, are what inspired some simplified characters, and for others, some were already in use in print text, albeit not for most formal works. In the period of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Republican China</a>, discussions on character simplification took place within the <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Kuomintang</a> government and the intelligentsia, in an effort to greatly reduce functional illiteracy among adults, which was a major concern at the time. Indeed, this desire by the Kuomintang to simplify the Chinese writing system (inherited and implemented by the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chinese Communist Party">Chinese Communist Party</a> after its subsequent abandonment) also nursed aspirations of some for the adoption of a phonetic script based on the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>, and spawned such inventions as the <a href="/wiki/Gwoyeu_Romatzyh" title="Gwoyeu Romatzyh">Gwoyeu Romatzyh</a>.
</p><p>The People's Republic of China issued its first round of official character simplifications in two documents, the first in 1956 and the second in 1964. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second-round_simplified_Chinese_character" title="Second-round simplified Chinese character">second round of character simplifications</a> (known as <span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn">erjian</i></span>, or "second-round simplified characters") was promulgated in 1977. It was poorly received, and in 1986 the authorities rescinded the second round completely, while making six revisions to the 1964 list, including the restoration of three traditional characters that had been simplified: <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">叠</span></span> <i>dié</i>, <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">覆</span></span> <i>fù</i>, <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">像</span></span> <i>xiàng</i>.
</p><p>As opposed to the second round, a majority of simplified characters in the first round were drawn from conventional abbreviated forms, or ancient forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey1987147_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey1987147-109">[103]</a></sup> For example, the orthodox character <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">來</span></span> <i>lái</i> ("come") was written as <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">来</span></span> in the <a href="/wiki/Clerical_script" title="Clerical script">clerical script</a> (<span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">隶书</span></span> / <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">隸書</span></span>, <i>lìshū</i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>. This clerical form uses one fewer stroke, and was thus adopted as a simplified form. The character <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">雲</span></span> <i>yún</i> ("cloud") was written with the structure <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">云</span></span> in the <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">oracle bone script</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>, and had remained in use later as a phonetic loan in the meaning of "to say" while the <span title="Chinese-language text"><span lang="zh">雨</span></span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_character)" title="Radical (Chinese character)">radical</a> was added a semantic indicator to disambiguate the two. Simplified Chinese simply merges them.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Japan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">Kanji</a>, <a href="/wiki/T%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Tōyō kanji">Tōyō kanji</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">Jōyō kanji</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shinjitai" title="Shinjitai">Shinjitai</a></div>
<p>In the years after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_government" title="Japanese government">Japanese government</a> also instituted a series of orthographic reforms. Some characters were given simplified forms called <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Shinjitai" title="Shinjitai">shinjitai</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">新字体</span></span>, lit. "new character forms")</span>; the older forms were then labelled the <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Ky%C5%ABjitai" title="Kyūjitai">kyūjitai</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">旧字体</span></span>, lit. "old character forms")</span>. The number of characters in common use was restricted, and formal lists of characters to be learned during each grade of school were established, first the 1850-character <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/T%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Tōyō kanji">tōyō kanji</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">当用漢字</span></span>)</span> list in 1945, the 1945-character <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">jōyō kanji</a></i></span><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">常用漢字</span></span>)</span> list in 1981, and a 2136-character reformed version of the <i>jōyō kanji</i> in 2010. Many variant forms of characters and obscure alternatives for common characters were officially discouraged. This was done with the goal of facilitating learning for children and simplifying kanji use in literature and periodicals. These are simply guidelines, hence many characters outside these standards are still widely known and commonly used, especially those used for personal and place names (for the latter, see <a href="/wiki/Jinmeiy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jinmeiyō kanji">jinmeiyō kanji</a>),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> as well as for some common words such as "dragon"<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">竜/龍</span></span>, <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">tatsu</i></span>)</span> in which both old and new forms of the character are both acceptable and widely known amongst native Japanese speakers.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Singapore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Singapore_Chinese_characters" title="Singapore Chinese characters">Singapore Chinese characters</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> underwent three successive rounds of character simplification. These resulted in some simplifications that differed from those used in <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>.
</p><p>The first round, consisting of 498 Simplified characters from 502 Traditional characters, was promulgated by the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education_(Singapore)" title="Ministry of Education (Singapore)">Ministry of Education</a> in 1969. The second round, consisting of 2287 Simplified characters, was promulgated in 1974. The second set contained 49 differences from the Mainland China system; those were removed in the final round in 1976.
</p><p>In 1993, Singapore adopted the six revisions made by Mainland China in 1986. However, unlike in mainland China where personal names may only be registered using simplified characters, parents have the option of registering their children's names in traditional characters in Singapore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>It ultimately adopted the reforms of the People's Republic of China in their entirety as official, and has implemented them in the <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Singapore" title="Education in Singapore">educational system</a>. However, unlike in China, personal names may still be registered in traditional characters.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaysia">Malaysia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Malaysia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> started teaching a set of simplified characters at schools in 1981, which were also completely identical to the Mainland China simplifications. Chinese newspapers in Malaysia are published in either set of characters, typically with the headlines in traditional Chinese while the body is in simplified Chinese.
</p><p>Although in both countries the use of simplified characters is universal among the younger Chinese generation, a large majority of the older Chinese literate generation still use the traditional characters. Chinese shop signs are also generally written in traditional characters.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, most Chinese schools and businesses still use the traditional characters and <a href="/wiki/Bopomofo" title="Bopomofo">bopomofo</a>, owing from influence from the Republic of China (Taiwan) due to the shared <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a> heritage. Recently, however, more Chinese schools now use both simplified characters and <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>. Since most readers of Chinese newspapers in the Philippines belong to the older generation, they are still published largely using traditional characters.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Canada_.26_United_States"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada_&amp;_United_States">Canada &amp; United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Canada &amp; United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Public and private Chinese signage in the United States and Canada most often use traditional characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup> There is some effort to get municipal governments to implement more simplified character signage due to recent immigration from mainland China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup> Most community newspapers printed in North America are also printed in traditional characters.
</p>
<h3><span id="Comparisons_of_traditional_Chinese.2C_simplified_Chinese.2C_and_Japanese"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparisons_of_traditional_Chinese,_simplified_Chinese,_and_Japanese">Comparisons of traditional Chinese, simplified Chinese, and Japanese</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chinese_characters&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Comparisons of traditional Chinese, simplified Chinese, and Japanese">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following is a comparison of Chinese characters in the <i><a href="/wiki/Standard_Form_of_National_Characters" title="Standard Form of National Characters">Standard Form of National Characters</a></i>, a common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese" title="Traditional Chinese">traditional Chinese</a> standard used in Taiwan; the <i><a href="/wiki/Table_of_General_Standard_Chinese_Characters" title="Table of General Standard Chinese Characters">Table of General Standard Chinese Characters</a></i>, the standard for Mainland Chinese <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters"><i>jiantizi</i></a> (simplified); and the <i><a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dy%C5%8D_kanji" title="Jōyō kanji">jōyō kanji</a></i>, the standard for Japanese <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a>. Generally, the <i>jōyō kanji</i> are more similar to <i>fantizi</i> (traditional) than <i>jiantizi</i> are to <i>fantizi</i>. "Simplified" refers to having significant differences from the Taiwan standard, not necessarily being a newly created character or a newly performed substitution. The characters in the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Graphemes_of_Commonly-Used_Chinese_Characters" title="List of Graphemes of Commonly-Used Chinese Characters">Hong Kong standard</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Dictionary" title="Kangxi Dictionary">Kangxi Dictionary</a> are also known as "Traditional", but are not shown.
</p>
